Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Jabriyya's Determinism Leads to Arbitrary Divine Will
Author's third objection: Jabriyya's view implies arbitrary divine decrees and negates the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Jabriyya's denial of moral good/evil and exclusive divine causality leads to arbitrary divine decrees and ultimately negates the Creator.

Core Idea
Denying human agency makes divine commands arbitrary and God's existence unnecessary.

The author presents a third powerful objection against the Jabriyya determinists. If only God creates all actions, including disbelief and faith, then there is no basis for distinguishing between them – it becomes an arbitrary assignment without reason. Moreover, by denying rational moral values, the Jabriyya make even divine commandments arbitrary, which contradicts the principle of causality and leads to the absurd conclusion that things can exist without a cause, ultimately negating God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Pay attention to the two 'if...then' structures.
2
Identify the two Jabriyya claims
The author attributes two core claims to the Jabriyya. Find them in the passage: one about causation, the other about morality.
3
Understand 'specification without a specifier'
The author argues that each claim leads to 'takhṣīṣ lā li-mukhaṣṣiṣ' – an arbitrary assignment without a reason. Consider why this is problematic.
This concept is central to the objection.
4
Trace the conclusion to negation of the Creator
The author says accepting arbitrary specification leads to the absurdity that a possible thing exists without a preponderant cause, which negates God. Follow that logic.
5
Note the author's claim of originality
The author states these are his own formulations and that no opponent has addressed them. Reflect on the significance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : إن مذهب الجبرية : أنه لا مؤثر في حدوث الممكنات إلا الله ، وإذا كان كذلك ، كان تخصيص بعض الناس بخلق الكفر والمعصية فيه ، والبعض الآخر بخلق الإيمان والطاعة . تخصيصاً لا لمخصص . وأيضاً : مذهب الجبرية : أنه لا حسن ولا قبح في العقول . وعلى هذا التقدير تخصيص بعض الأفعال بالإيجاب ، والبعض بالندب ، والبعض بالتحريم : تخصيصاً لا لمخصص أصلا . ومجوز هذا ، يلزمه أن يحكم باستغناء الممكن عن المرجح . وذلك يقتضي نفي الصانع . وهذان الوجهان ، نحن الخصناهما للقوم . وما رأينا أحداً منهم دار حولهما .
The third aspect: Indeed, the doctrine of the Jabriyya is that there is no effect in the occurrence of any possibilities except God. And if it is so, then the specification of some people to create disbelief and disobedience in them, and others to create faith and obedience, is a specification without a specifier. Also: the doctrine of the Jabriyya is that there is no good nor evil in the intellects. And upon this estimation, the specification of some actions as obligatory, some as recommended, and some as forbidden, is a specification without a specifier at all. And the one who permits this would be forced to judge that a possible is independent of a preponderant, and that entails the negation of the Creator. These two aspects we have formulated exclusively for the people [i.e., the Mu'tazila?], and I have not seen any of them circling around them.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the author's third objection against the Jabriyya (determinists). The author argues that if only God creates all events, including human actions, then there is no basis for distinguishing between the creation of faith and disbelief in different people—it becomes an arbitrary assignment without a reason (takhṣīṣ lā li-mukhaṣṣiṣ). Moreover, the Jabriyya deny rational moral values (no good or evil in the intellect), so the divine commands themselves—what God obligates, recommends, or forbids—also become arbitrary, without any rational ground. This leads to the absurd conclusion that a possible thing (like a human action or a divine command) could exist without a preponderant cause (murajjiḥ), which contradicts the necessity of a cause and ultimately negates the existence of the Creator. The author notes that these two objections (the second and third) are his own formulation, and he claims no opponent has addressed them.

Key Terms
لا مؤثر
تخصيص لا لمخصص
حسن وقبح عقلي
استغناء الممكن عن المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jabriyya think that denying human agency exalts God's power.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to irrationality, arbitrary divine decrees, and ultimately negates God's wisdom and even His existence.

Checkpoint
According to the author's third objection, what is the consequence of the Jabriyya denial of rational good and evil?
A) It proves that God is just.
B) It makes divine commandments arbitrary and allows for possibilities without a cause, which implies the negation of God.
C) It shows that people are responsible for their actions.
D) It establishes the necessity of prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya doctrine, by denying human causality and rational morality, makes God's actions arbitrary and ultimately contradicts the principle of causality, negating the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every action you perform is directly created by God, how would you face Him on the Day of Judgment when asked about your sins? Would you claim that He forced you, or does your conscience hold you accountable? Let this question drive you to examine the choices you make today.
?
The author argues that denying human agency makes God's commands arbitrary. Think of a command you frequently struggle with. In your heart, do you sometimes excuse your disobedience by thinking 'It was written'? How does this lesson expose the danger of that excuse? Commit now to take full responsibility for your next moment of weakness.
?
Imagine standing before Allah knowing that He commanded you to pray, yet you delayed. If the Jabriyya view were true, your delay would be created directly by God. Does that align with your sense of His justice and wisdom? Let this reflection push you to thank Allah for granting you free will and to use it in obedience.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Refuting the Argument from Human Actions for Contingency
The author refutes an argument by a dilemma and a methodological point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma used to refute an argument for the world's contingency based on human actions, and the methodological point that a single invalid proof does not disprove a conclusion.

Core Idea
The occurrence of actions after intention does not prove causation—coincidence is possible.

This passage presents a powerful refutation of an argument often used by theologians who claim that our ability to perform actions demonstrates that the world is contingent and requires a creator. The author, likely a classical scholar, dismantles this argument by presenting a dilemma: either the dependence of the contingent is known, making the argument redundant, or it is unknown, leaving room for coincidence. Additionally, he makes a crucial methodological point that even a flawed proof does not invalidate the conclusion itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the dilemma
Carefully read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two branches of the dilemma: either the dependence of the contingent is known or unknown.
The passage begins with 'وذلك لأن افتقار الحادث إلى المؤثر. إما أن يكون معلوماً ، أو لا يكون .'
2
Analyze each branch
Understand the consequences of each branch: if known, the argument from actions is unnecessary; if unknown, actions may be coincidental.
3
Consider the second methodological point
The author adds that even if the argument were valid, its invalidity does not disprove the conclusion, as it could be established by another proof.
This is a separate but important logical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصانع . وذلك لأن افتقار الحادث إلى المؤثر. إما أن يكون معلوماً ، أو لا يكون . فإن كان معلوما ، لزم من العلم يكون العالم محدثاً : العلم بافتقاره إلى المؤثر، من غير حاجة إلى نفيه، على افتقارنا إلينا، وإن كان غير معلوم، يلزم من مشاهدة وقوع أفعالنا، عقيب تصورنا ودواعينا : وقوعها . لاحتمال أن تلك الأفعال وقعت عندها، لا بها، ولا بشيء آخر . بل حدثت على سبيل الاتفاق . فثبت : أن ما ذكرتموه ليس دليلا صحيحاً . الثاني : هب أن ما ذكرتم دليل صحيح . إلا أنه لا يلزم من بطلان دليل واحد، بطلان القول بالمدلول . لاحتمال أن يثبت ذلك المدلول بدليل آخر .
The Maker. And that is because the dependence of the contingent upon the effect: either it is known or it is not. If it is known, then from the knowledge that the world is contingent follows the knowledge of its dependence upon the effect, without need to negate it, upon our dependence on us. And if it is not known, then from observing the occurrence of our actions after our concepts and motives, their occurrence follows, because it is possible that those actions occurred with them, not by them, nor by anything else. Rather they occurred by way of coincidence. So it is established that what you have mentioned is not a sound proof. Second: Suppose that what you mentioned is a sound proof. However, from the invalidity of one proof, the invalidity of the claimed conclusion does not follow, because it is possible that that conclusion is established by another proof.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that from the occurrence of human actions after intention and motive, one can prove that the world is contingent and requires an external cause. The author presents a dilemma: either the dependence of the contingent on an effect is known or it is not known. If it is known, then we already know the world's contingency and its need for a cause without needing to consider our actions. If it is not known, then observing our actions after our intentions does not prove causation—they might simply coincide. Thus the argument fails. The author then adds a second point: even if the argument were valid, the invalidity of a single proof does not disprove the conclusion, which could be established by another proof.

Key Terms
افتقار الحادث
المؤثر
دليل
مدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author is denying any causal connection between intentions and actions or denying human agency.
Correction: The author is only refuting a specific argument that uses human actions to prove the world's contingency; he is not denying the causal relationship itself.

Checkpoint
What is the author's first point in refuting the argument from human actions?
A) The argument is circular.
B) The argument is based on unknown premises.
C) The argument either leads to unnecessary proof or coincidence.
D) The argument is invalid because it negates divine agency.

Key Takeaway
The argument from human actions to prove the world's contingency is refuted by a dilemma: either it is redundant or it relies on a potential coincidence. Additionally, even a valid proof's invalidity does not negate the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you so attached to your own reasoning that you would reject a truth simply because your argument for it was dismantled? When has your pride in being 'rational' prevented you from accepting something you could not prove?
?
You rely every day on cause and effect—but have you ever considered that your confidence in these patterns might be based on a kind of intellectual laziness? What would it cost you to admit that some of your deepest convictions might be built on coincidence rather than proof?
?
If the invalidity of one argument does not disprove the conclusion, then your personal failure to defend a belief does not make it false. Whose opinion are you holding to because you lack the skill to argue it, and what would it take for you to seek a better proof?
